<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Adi Shankaracharya" redirects here. <div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:#FFC569;;font-weight:normal;"><div class="sidebar-list-title-c"><b><a href="/wiki/Hindu_texts" title="Hindu texts">Major texts</a></b></div></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><div class="hlist hlist-separated">

<dl><dd><b><a href="/wiki/Secular_ethics" title="Secular ethics">Secular ethics</a></b></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tirukkural" title="Tirukkural">Tirukkural</a></li></ul>
</div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Other Indian philosophies</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Hindu_philosophy" title="Template:Hindu philosophy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Hindu_philosophy" title="Template talk:Hindu philosophy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Adi Shankara</b> (8th cent. CE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[note 1]</a></sup> also called <b>Adi Shankaracharya</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">आदि शङ्कर, आदि शङ्कराचार्य</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Sanskrit" title="Romanization of Sanskrit">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Sanskrit-language romanization"><i lang="sa-Latn">Ādi Śaṅkarācāryaḥ</i></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'First <a href="/wiki/Shankaracharya" title="Shankaracharya">Shankara</a>', <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="sa-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Sanskrit" title="Help:IPA/Sanskrit">[aːdɪ ɕɐŋkɐraːtɕaːrjɐh]</a></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[note 2]</a></sup> was an <a href="/wiki/Indian_people" title="Indian people">Indian</a> <a href="/wiki/Vedanga" title="Vedanga">Vedic scholar</a> and teacher (<i><a href="/wiki/Acharya" title="Acharya">acharya</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuthren_Hirst20051_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuthren_Hirst20051-6">[4]</a></sup> whose works present a harmonizing reading of the <i>sastras</i>, with liberating knowledge of the self at its core, synthesizing the <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a> teachings of his time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004678–679_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004678–679-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_Dalal2021_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford_Dalal2021-8">[web 1]</a></sup> The title of <a href="/wiki/Shankaracharya" title="Shankaracharya">Shankracharya</a>, used by heads of the <a href="/wiki/Amnaya" title="Amnaya">amnaya</a> monasteries is derived from his name.
</p><p>Due to his later fame, over 300 texts are attributed to his name, including commentaries (<i>Bhāṣya</i>), introductory topical expositions (<i>Prakaraṇa grantha</i>) and poetry (<i>Stotra</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva19932–3_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva19932–3-10">[7]</a></sup> However most of these are likely to be by admirers or pretenders or scholars with an eponymous name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31-11">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halbfass1983_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-halbfass1983-12">[9]</a></sup> Authentic are the <i>Brahmasutrabhasya</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7-9">[6]</a></sup> his commentaries on ten <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mukhya_Upanishads" title="Mukhya Upanishads">Mukhya</a> (principal) Upanishads,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31-11">[8]</a></sup> his commentary on the <a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita" title="Bhagavad Gita">Bhagavad Gita</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1991xii–xiii_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1991xii–xiii-13">[10]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Upadesasahasri" title="Upadesasahasri">Upadesasahasri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halbfassyoga_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-halbfassyoga-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup> The authenticity of Shankara being the author of <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viveka_Chudamani" title="Viveka Chudamani">Vivekacūḍāmaṇi</a></i></span> has been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes2004_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes2004-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShah-Kazemi20064_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShah-Kazemi20064-17">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The central postulation of Shankara's writings is the identity of the Self (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Ātman</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1999176_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1999176-18">[15]</a></sup> defending the liberating knowledge of the Self, taking the Upanishads as an independent means of knowledge, against the ritually-oriented <a href="/wiki/M%C4%ABm%C4%81%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Mīmāṃsā">Mīmāṃsā</a> school of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000163_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000163-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKChattopadhyaya_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-SKChattopadhyaya-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ritualism_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-ritualism-20">[note 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mookerji_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mookerji-21">[note 4]</a></sup> Shankara's Advaita shows influences from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahayana_Buddhism" title="Mahayana Buddhism">Mahayana Buddhism</a>, despite Shankara's critiques;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shcherbatsky_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shcherbatsky-22">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDasgupta1997494_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDasgupta1997494-23">[18]</a></sup> and Hindu <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavist</a> opponents have even accused Shankara of being a "crypto-Buddhist,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiderman1978405–413_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiderman1978405–413-24">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isaeva14_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-isaeva14-25">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1995183_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1995183-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crypto-Buddhist_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-crypto-Buddhist-27">[note 5]</a></sup> a qualification which is rejected by the Advaita Vedanta tradition, highlighting their respective views on <i>Atman</i>, <i>Anatta</i> and <i>Brahman</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199360,_145–154_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199360,_145–154-28">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-self_no-self_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-self_no-self-29">[note 6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankara has an unparallelled status in the tradition of Advaita Vedanta,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000163_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000163-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma1962vi_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1962vi-30">[23]</a></sup> but his influence on Hindu intellectual thought has been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200229_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200229-31">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2001128_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2001128-32">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETola1989_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETola1989-33">[26]</a></sup> 
Until the 10th century Shankara was overshadowed by his older contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ma%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8Dana_Mi%C5%9Bra" title="Maṇḍana Miśra">Mandana-Misra</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2001128_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2001128-32">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34-34">[27]</a></sup> and there is no mention of him in concurring Hindu, Buddhist or Jain sources until the 11th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark2006217_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark2006217-35">[28]</a></sup> The popular image Shankara started to take shape only in the 14th century, centuries after his death, when Sringeri <i>matha</i> started to receive patronage from the kings of the Vijayanagara Empire<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34-34">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30-36">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodding201389_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodding201389-37">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blakemichael_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-blakemichael-38">[31]</a></sup> and shifted their allegiance from <i>advaitic</i> Agamic Saivism to Brahmanical Advaita orthodoxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark2006215,_221-222_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark2006215,_221-222-39">[32]</a></sup> Hagiographies dating from the 14th-17th centuries deified him as a <a href="/wiki/Chakravarti_(Sanskrit_term)" title="Chakravarti (Sanskrit term)">ruler</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sannyasin" title="Sannyasin">renunciate</a>, travelling on a <a href="/wiki/Digvijaya_(conquest)" title="Digvijaya (conquest)">digvijaya</a> (conquest of the four quarters)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowicka2016147_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowicka2016147-40">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBader2001vii_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBader2001vii-41">[34]</a></sup> across the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> to propagate his philosophy, defeating his opponents in theological debates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raju1985_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raju1985-42">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[36]</a></sup> These hagiographies portray him as founding four <a href="#Mathas">mathas</a> ("monasteries"), and Adi Shankara also came to be regarded as the organiser of the <a href="/wiki/Da%C5%9Ban%C4%81mi_Sampradaya" title="Daśanāmi Sampradaya">Dashanami</a> monastic order, and the unifier of the <a href="/wiki/Shanmata" title="Shanmata">Shanmata</a> tradition of worship.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dating">Dating</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaladi_shankarabirthplace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="203" data-file-width="343" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Kaladi_shankarabirthplace.jpg/220px-Kaladi_shankarabirthplace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Kaladi_shankarabirthplace.jpg/330px-Kaladi_shankarabirthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/45/Kaladi_shankarabirthplace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaladi_shankarabirthplace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The birthplace of Adi Shankara at <a href="/wiki/Kalady" title="Kalady">Kalady</a></div></div></div>
<p>Reliable information on Shankara's actual life is scanty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82-44">[37]</a></sup> His existing biographies were all written several centuries after his time and abound in legends and improbable events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britshankaradates_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-britshankaradates-45">[38]</a></sup> The records of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sringeri_matha" title="Sringeri matha">Sringeri Matha</a> state that Shankara was born in the 14th year of the reign of "Vikramaditya", but it is unclear to which king this name refers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nila_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-nila-46">[39]</a></sup> Though some researchers identify the name with <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_II" title="Chandragupta II">Chandragupta II</a> (4th century CE), modern scholarship accepts the Vikramaditya as being from the <a href="/wiki/Chalukya_dynasty" title="Chalukya dynasty">Chalukya dynasty of Badami</a>, most likely <a href="/wiki/Vikramaditya_II" title="Vikramaditya II">Vikramaditya II</a> (733–746 CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nila_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-nila-46">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several different dates have been proposed for Shankara:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199383–87_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199383–87-47">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>509–477 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a>: This dating is based on records of the heads of the Shankara's cardinal institutions <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Maṭha</i></span>s. The exact dates of birth of Adi Shankaracharya believed by four monasteries are <a href="/wiki/Dv%C4%81rak%C4%81" title="Dvārakā">Dvārakā</a> at 491 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[note 7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joshimath#Religious_significance" title="Joshimath">Jyotirmath</a> at 485 BCE, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jagannatha_Puri" title="Jagannatha Puri">Jagannatha Puri</a> at 484 BCE and <a href="/wiki/Sringeri" title="Sringeri">Sringeri</a> at 483 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-51">[43]</a></sup> while according to the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kanchi_Peetham&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kanchi Peetham (page does not exist)">Kanchi Peetham</a> Adi Shankara was born in Kali 2593 (509 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalal2010p376_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dalal2010p376-52">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[note 8]</a></sup></li>
<li>44–12 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a>: the commentator Anandagiri believed he was born at Chidambaram in 44 BCE and died in 12 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keshava_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keshava-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>6th century CE: Telang placed him in this century. Sir R.G. Bhandarkar believed he was born in 680 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keshava_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keshava-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 700</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 750</span> CE: Late 20th-century and early 21st-century scholarship tends to place Shankara's life of 32 years in the first half of the 8th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britshankaradates_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-britshankaradates-45">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[47]</a></sup> According to the Indologist and Asian Religions scholar John Koller, there is considerable controversy regarding the dates of Shankara – widely regarded as one of India's greatest thinkers, and "the best recent scholarship argues that he was born in 700 and died in 750 CE".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeisterCopan2013_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-MeisterCopan2013-1">[1]</a></sup></li>
<li>788–820 CE: This was proposed by early twentieth century scholars and was customarily accepted by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max Müller">Max Müller</a>, Macdonnel, Pathok, Deussen and Radhakrishna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keshava_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Keshava-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madhava_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madhava-58">[49]</a></sup> The date 788–820 is also among those considered acceptable by <a href="/wiki/Swami_Tapasyananda_(Ramakrishna_Mission)" title="Swami Tapasyananda (Ramakrishna Mission)">Swami Tapasyananda</a>, though he raises a number of questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[50]</a></sup> Though the 788–820 CE dates are widespread in 20th-century publications, recent scholarship has questioned the 788–820 CE dates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britshankaradates_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-britshankaradates-45">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>805–897 CE: Venkiteswara not only places Shankara later than most, but also had the opinion that it would not have been possible for him to have achieved all the works apportioned to him, and has him live ninety-two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keshava_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Keshava-3">[3]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The popularly-accepted dating places Shankara to be a scholar from the first half of the 8th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000163_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000163-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5-60">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara_bibliography" title="Adi Shankara bibliography">Adi Shankara bibliography</a></div>
<p>Adi Shankara's works are the foundation of Advaita Vedanta school of Hinduism, and his doctrine, states Sengaku Mayeda, "has been the source from which the main currents of modern Indian thought are derived".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7-9">[6]</a></sup> Over 300 texts are attributed to his name, including commentaries (<i>Bhāṣya</i>), original philosophical expositions (<i>Prakaraṇa grantha</i>) and poetry (<i>Stotra</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva19932–3_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva19932–3-10">[7]</a></sup> However most of these are not authentic works of Shankara and are likely to be by his admirers or scholars whose name was also Shankaracharya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31-11">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halbfass1983_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-halbfass1983-12">[9]</a></sup> Piantelli has published a complete list of works attributed to Adi Sankara, along with issues of authenticity for most.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authentic_works">Authentic works</span></h3>
<p>Shankara is most known for his systematic reviews and commentaries (<i>Bhasyas</i>) on ancient Indian texts. Shankara's masterpiece of commentary is the <i>Brahmasutrabhasya</i> (literally, commentary on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_Sutra" title="Brahma Sutra">Brahma Sutra</a>), a fundamental text of the <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> school of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7-9">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>His commentaries on ten <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mukhya_Upanishads" title="Mukhya Upanishads">Mukhya</a> (principal) Upanishads are also considered authentic by scholars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31-11">[8]</a></sup> and these are: <i>Bhasya</i> on the <a href="/wiki/Brihadaranyaka_Upanishad" title="Brihadaranyaka Upanishad">Brihadaranyaka Upanishad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chandogya_Upanishad" title="Chandogya Upanishad">Chandogya Upanishad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aitareya_Upanishad" title="Aitareya Upanishad">Aitareya Upanishad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Taittiriya_Upanishad" title="Taittiriya Upanishad">Taittiriya Upanishad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kena_Upanishad" title="Kena Upanishad">Kena Upanishad</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[note 9]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Isha_Upanishad" title="Isha Upanishad">Isha Upanishad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Katha_Upanishad" title="Katha Upanishad">Katha Upanishad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mundaka_Upanishad" title="Mundaka Upanishad">Mundaka Upanishad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Prashna_Upanishad" title="Prashna Upanishad">Prashna Upanishad</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mandukya_Upanishad" title="Mandukya Upanishad">Mandukya Upanishad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199393–97_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199393–97-64">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113-65">[55]</a></sup> Of these, the commentary on Mandukya, is actually a commentary on Madukya-Karikas by <a href="/wiki/Gaudapada" title="Gaudapada">Gaudapada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113-65">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other authentic works of Shankara include commentaries on the <a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita" title="Bhagavad Gita">Bhagavad Gita</a> (part of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prasthana_Trayi" title="Prasthana Trayi">Prasthana Trayi</a> Bhasya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1991xii–xiii_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1991xii–xiii-13">[10]</a></sup> His <i>Vivarana</i> (tertiary notes) on the commentary by Vedavyasa on <a href="/wiki/Yoga_Sutras_of_Patanjali" title="Yoga Sutras of Patanjali">Yogasutras</a> as well as those on Apastamba Dharma-sũtras (<i>Adhyatama-patala-bhasya</i>) are accepted by scholars as authentic works of Shankara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199393–97_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199393–97-64">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halbfassyoga_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-halbfassyoga-14">[11]</a></sup> Among the <i>Stotra</i> (poetic works), the Daksinamurti Stotra, the Bhajagovinda Stotra, the Sivanandalahari, the Carpata-panjarika, the Visnu-satpadi, the Harimide, the Dasa-shloki, and the Krishna-staka are likely to be authentic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199393–97_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199393–97-64">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011351–352_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011351–352-66">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankara also authored <a href="/wiki/Upadesasahasri" title="Upadesasahasri">Upadesasahasri</a>, his most important original philosophical work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halbfassyoga_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-halbfassyoga-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup> Of other original <i>Prakaranas</i> (प्रकरण, monographs, treatise), seventy-six works are attributed to Shankara. Modern era Indian scholars such as Belvalkar as well as Upadhyaya accept five and thirty-nine works respectively as authentic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011113–115_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011113–115-67">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankara's stotras considered authentic include those dedicated to Krishna (<a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a>) and one to Shiva (<a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shaivism</a>) – often considered two different sects within Hinduism. Scholars suggest that these <i>stotra</i> are not sectarian, but essentially Advaitic and reach for a unified universal view of Vedanta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011351–352_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011351–352-66">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankara's commentary on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_Sutra" title="Brahma Sutra">Brahma Sutras</a> is the oldest surviving. However, in that commentary, he mentions older commentaries like those of Dravida, Bhartrprapancha and others which are either lost or yet to be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Works_of_doubtful_authenticity_or_not_authentic">Works of doubtful authenticity or not authentic</span></h3>
<p>Commentaries on Nrisimha-Purvatatapaniya and Shveshvatara Upanishads are attributed to Shankara, but their authenticity is highly doubtful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199530–31-11">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113-65">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[59]</a></sup> Similarly, commentaries on several early and later Upanishads attributed to Shankara are rejected by scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paulhackeraupr_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-paulhackeraupr-70">[60]</a></sup> to be his works, and are likely works of later scholars; these include: Kaushitaki Upanishad, Maitri Upanishad, Kaivalya Upanishad, Paramahamsa Upanishad, Sakatayana Upanishad, Mandala Brahmana Upanishad, Maha Narayana Upanishad, Gopalatapaniya Upanishad. However, in Brahmasutra-Bhasya, Shankara cites some of these Upanishads as he develops his arguments, but the historical notes left by his companions and disciples, along with major differences in style and the content of the commentaries on later Upanishad have led scholars to conclude that the commentaries on later Upanishads were not Shankara's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113-65">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The authenticity of Shankara being the author of <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viveka_Chudamani" title="Viveka Chudamani">Vivekacūḍāmaṇi</a></i></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[61]</a></sup> has been questioned,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes2004_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes2004-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShah-Kazemi20064_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShah-Kazemi20064-17">[14]</a></sup> though it is "so closely interwoven into the spiritual heritage of Shankara that any analysis of his perspective which fails to consider [this work] would be incomplete."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShah-Kazemi20064_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShah-Kazemi20064-17">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[note 10]</a></sup> According to Grimes, "modern scholars tend to reject its authenticity as a work by Shankara," while "traditionalists tend to accept it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes200423_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes200423-73">[62]</a></sup> Nevertheless, does Grimes argue that "there is still a likelihood that Śaṅkara is the author of the Vivekacūḍāmaṇi," <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes200423_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes200423-73">[62]</a></sup> noting that "it differs in certain respects from his other works in that it addresses itself to a different audience and has a different emphasis and purpose."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes200413_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes200413-74">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Aparokshanubhuti" title="Aparokshanubhuti">Aparokshanubhuti</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Atma_bodha" title="Atma bodha">Atma bodha</a></i> are also attributed to Shankara, as his original philosophical treatises, but this is doubtful. Paul Hacker has also expressed some reservations that the compendium <i><a href="/wiki/Sarva-siddhanta_Sangraha" title="Sarva-siddhanta Sangraha">Sarva-darsana-siddhanta Sangraha</a></i> was completely authored by Shankara, because of difference in style and thematic inconsistencies in parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paulhackeraupr_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-paulhackeraupr-70">[60]</a></sup> Similarly, <i>Gayatri-bhasya</i> is doubtful to be Shankara's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113_65-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113-65">[55]</a></sup> Other commentaries that are highly unlikely to be Shankara's work include those on <i>Uttaragita</i>, <i>Siva-gita</i>, <i>Brahma-gita</i>, <i>Lalita-shasranama</i>, <i>Suta-samhita</i> and <i>Sandhya-bhasya</i>. The commentary on the Tantric work <i>Lalita-trisati-bhasya</i> attributed to Shankara is also unauthentic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113_65-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113-65">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankara is widely credited with commentaries on other scriptural works, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vishnu_sahasranama" title="Vishnu sahasranama">Vishnu sahasranāma</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sanatsujatiya" title="Sanatsujatiya">Sānatsujātiya</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanb78_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanb78-75">[64]</a></sup> but both these are considered apocryphal by scholars who have expressed doubts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113_65-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113-65">[55]</a></sup> <i>Hastamalakiya-bhasya</i> is also widely believed in India to be Shankara's work and it is included in <i>Samata</i>-edition of Shankara's works, but some scholars consider it to be the work of Shankara's student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113_65-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande2011105–113-65">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_and_practice">Philosophy and practice</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30em; ; background-color: #FFE0BB;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><a href="/wiki/Atma_Shatkam" title="Atma Shatkam">Atma Shatkam (The song of the Self)</a>:
</p><p>I am Consciousness, I am <a href="/wiki/Satcitananda" title="Satcitananda">Bliss</a>, I am Shiva, I am Shiva.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[note 11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Without hate, without infatuation, without craving, without greed;<br/>
Neither arrogance, nor conceit, never jealous I am;<br/>
Neither <i>dharma</i>, nor <i>artha</i>, neither <i>kama</i>, nor <i>moksha</i> am I;<br/>
I am Consciousness, I am Bliss, I am Shiva, I am Shiva.
</p><p>Without sins, without merits, without elation, without sorrow;<br/>
Neither mantra, nor rituals, neither pilgrimage, nor Vedas;<br/>
Neither the experiencer, nor experienced, nor the experience am I,<br/>
I am Consciousness, I am Bliss, I am Shiva, I am Shiva.
</p><p>Without fear, without death, without discrimination, without caste;<br/>
Neither father, nor mother, never born I am;<br/>
Neither kith, nor kin, neither teacher, nor student am I;<br/>
I am Consciousness, I am Bliss, I am Shiva, I am Shiva.
</p><p>Without form, without figure, without resemblance am I;<br/>
Vitality of all senses, in everything I am;<br/>
Neither attached, nor released am I;<br/>
I am Consciousness, I am Bliss, I am Shiva, I am Shiva.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—Adi Shankara, Nirvana Shatakam, Hymns 3–6<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[66]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>As per Nakamura, Shankara was not an original thinker, but systematised the works of preceding philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004680_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004680-79">[67]</a></sup> The central theme of Shankara's writings is the liberating knowledge of the identity of the Self (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atman_(Hinduism)" title="Atman (Hinduism)">Ātman</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1999176_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1999176-18">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">Moksha</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jivanmukti" title="Jivanmukti">attained in this life</a> by recognizing the identity of <i>Atman</i> and <i>Brahman</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup> as mediated by the <i><a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%81kyas" title="Mahāvākyas">Mahavakyas</a></i>, especially <i>Tat Tvam Asi</i>, "That you are."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Systematizer_of_Advaita">Systematizer of Advaita</span></h3>
<p>According to Nakamura, comparison of the known teachings of the early Vedantins and Shankara's thought shows that most of the characteristics of Shankara's thought "were advocated by someone before Śankara".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004678_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004678-80">[68]</a></sup> Shankara "was the person who synthesized the <i>Advaita-vāda</i> which had previously existed before him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004678_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004678-80">[68]</a></sup> According to Nakamura, after the growing influence of Buddhism on Vedānta, culminating in the works of Gauḍapāda, Adi Shankara gave a Vedantic character to the Buddhistic elements in these works,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200613_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200613-81">[69]</a></sup> synthesising and rejuvenating the doctrine of Advaita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004679_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004679-82">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Koller, using ideas in ancient Indian texts, Shankara systematized the foundation for Advaita <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedānta</a> in the 8th century, reforming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Badarayana" title="Badarayana">Badarayana</a>'s Vedānta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup> According to Mayeda, Shankara represents a turning point in the development of Vedānta,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200613_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200613-81">[69]</a></sup> yet he also notices that it is only since Deussens's praise that Shankara "has usually been regarded as the greatest philosopher of India."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992XV_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992XV-83">[71]</a></sup> Mayeda further notes that Shankara was primarily concerned with <i>moksha</i>, "and not with the establishment of a complete system of philosophy or theology,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992XV_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992XV-83">[71]</a></sup> following Potter, who qualifies Shankara as a "speculative philosopher."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992XVIII,_note_3_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992XVIII,_note_3-84">[72]</a></sup> Lipner notes that Shankara's "main literary approach was commentarial and hence perforce disjointed rather than procedurally systematic [...] though a systematic philosophy can be derived from Samkara's thought."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner200056,_incl._note_12_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner200056,_incl._note_12-85">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankara has been described as influenced by Shaivism and Shaktism, but his works and philosophy suggest greater overlap with Vaishnavism, influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_yoga" title="Raja yoga">Yoga</a> school of Hinduism, but most distinctly express his Advaitin convictions with a monistic view of spirituality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5-60">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva19933,_29–30_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva19933,_29–30-86">[74]</a></sup> and his commentaries mark a turn from realism to idealism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma200064_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma200064-87">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000123_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000123-88">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moksha_-_liberating_knowledge_of_Brahman"><i>Moksha</i> - liberating knowledge of Brahman</span></h3>
<p>The central theme of Shankara's writings is the identity of the Self (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atman_(Hinduism)" title="Atman (Hinduism)">Ātman</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1999176_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1999176-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[note 12]</a></sup> One of Shankara's main concerns was explaining the liberating knowledge of the Self, and defending the Upanishads as an independent means of knowledge against the ritually-oriented <a href="/wiki/M%C4%ABm%C4%81%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Mīmāṃsā">Mīmāṃsā</a> school of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKChattopadhyaya_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-SKChattopadhyaya-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000163_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000163-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ritualism_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-ritualism-20">[note 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mookerji_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mookerji-21">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Shankara, the one unchanging entity (Brahman) alone is real, while changing entities do not have absolute existence. Shankara's primary objective was to explain how <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a> is <a href="/wiki/Moksha#Mokṣha_in_this_life" title="Moksha">attained in this life</a> by recognizing the identity of <i>Atman</i> and <i>Brahman</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnkoller_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-johnkoller-15">[12]</a></sup> as mediated by the <i><a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%81kyas" title="Mahāvākyas">Mahāvākyas</a></i>, especially <i>Tat Tvam Asi</i>, "That you are." Correct knowledge of Atman and Brahman is the attainment of <i>Brahman</i>, immortality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan200626_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan200626-90">[77]</a></sup> and leads to <i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a></i> (liberation) from suffering<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[note 13]</a></sup> and <i>samsara</i>, the cycle of rebirth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000183_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000183-92">[78]</a></sup> This is stated by Shankara as follows:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="poem">
<p>I am other than name, form and action.<br/>
My nature is ever free!<br/>
I am Self, the supreme unconditioned Brahman.<br/>
I am pure Awareness, always non-dual.
</p>
</div>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Adi Shankara, <a href="/wiki/Upadesasahasri" title="Upadesasahasri">Upadesasahasri</a> 11.7, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000183_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000183-92">[78]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pramanas_-_means_of_knowledge"><i>Pramanas</i> - means of knowledge</span></h3>
<p>Shankara recognized the means of knowledge,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200646_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200646-93">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[note 14]</a></sup> but his thematic focus was upon <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soteriology" title="Soteriology">soteriology</a>, and he took for granted the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pramanas" title="Pramanas">pramanas</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200647_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200647-97">[82]</a></sup> that is <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> or "means to gain knowledge, reasoning methods that empower one to gain reliable knowledge".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> According to Sengaku Mayeda, "in no place in his works [...] does he give any systematic account of them,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200647_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200647-97">[82]</a></sup> taking <i>Atman-Brahman</i> to be self-evident (<i>svapramanaka</i>) and self-established (<i>svatahsiddha</i>), and "an investigation of the means of knowledge is of no use for the attainment of final release."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200647_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200647-97">[82]</a></sup> Mayeda notes that Shankara's arguments are "strikingly realistic and not idealistic," arguing that <i>jnana</i> is based on existing things (<i>vastutantra</i>), and "not upon Vedic injunction (<i>codanatantra</i>) nor upon man (<i>purusatantra</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200647_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200647-97">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Michael Comans (aka Vasudevacharya), Shankara considered perception and inference as a primary most reliable epistemic means, and where these means to knowledge help one gain "what is beneficial and to avoid what is harmful", there is no need for or wisdom in referring to the scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000168_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000168-98">[83]</a></sup> In certain matters related to metaphysics and ethics, says Shankara, the testimony and wisdom in scriptures such as the Vedas and the Upanishads become important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000167–169_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000167–169-99">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Merrell-Wolff states that Shankara accepts Vedas and Upanishads as a source of knowledge as he develops his philosophical theses, yet he never rests his case on the ancient texts, rather proves each thesis, point by point using the <i><a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">pramanas</a></i> (means of knowledge) of reason and experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[86]</a></sup> Hacker and Phillips note that his insight into rules of reasoning and hierarchical emphasis on epistemic steps is "doubtlessly the suggestion" of Shankara in Brahma-sutra-bhasya, an insight that flowers in the works of his companion and disciple Padmapada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic_versus_revelation">Logic versus revelation</span></h4>
<p>Stcherbatsky in 1927 criticized Shankara for demanding the use of logic from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madhyamika" title="Madhyamika">Madhyamika</a> Buddhists, while himself resorting to revelation as a source of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shcherbatsky_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shcherbatsky-22">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[note 15]</a></sup> Sircar in 1933 offered a different perspective and stated, "Sankara recognizes the value of the law of contrariety and self-alienation from the standpoint of idealistic logic; and it has consequently been possible for him to integrate appearance with reality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent scholarship states that Shankara's arguments on revelation are about <i>apta vacana</i> (Sanskrit: आप्तवचन, sayings of the wise, relying on word, testimony of past or present reliable experts).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arvindrevel_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-arvindrevel-105">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[90]</a></sup> It is part of his and Advaita Vedanta's epistemological foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arvindrevel_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-arvindrevel-105">[89]</a></sup> The Advaita Vedanta tradition considers such testimony epistemically valid, asserting that a human being needs to know numerous facts, and with the limited time and energy available, he can learn only a fraction of those facts and truths directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[91]</a></sup> Shankara considered the teachings in the Vedas and Upanishads as <i>apta vacana</i> and a valid source of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arvindrevel_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-arvindrevel-105">[89]</a></sup> He suggests the importance of teacher-disciple relationship on combining logic and revelation to attain <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a> in his text <a href="/wiki/Upadesasahasri" title="Upadesasahasri">Upadeshasahasri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva1993219–223_with_footnote_34_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva1993219–223_with_footnote_34-108">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anantanand_Rambachan" title="Anantanand Rambachan">Anantanand Rambachan</a> and others state that Shankara did not rely exclusively on Vedic statements, but also used a range of logical methods and reasoning methodology and other <i>pramanas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva1993210–221_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva1993210–221-109">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1991Chapters_2–4_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1991Chapters_2–4-110">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anubhava">Anubhava</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anantanand_Rambachan" title="Anantanand Rambachan">Anantanand Rambachan</a> summarizes the widely held view on the role of <i>anubhava</i> in Shankara's epistemology as follows, before critiquing it:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>According to these [widely represented contemporary] studies, Shankara only accorded a provisional validity to the knowledge gained by inquiry into the words of the <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aruti" title="Śruti">Śruti</a> (Vedas) and did not see the latter as the unique source (<i>pramana</i>) of <i>Brahmajnana</i>. The affirmations of the Śruti, it is argued, need to be verified and confirmed by the knowledge gained through direct experience (<i>anubhava</i>) and the authority of the Śruti, therefore, is only secondary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1991xii–xiii_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1991xii–xiii-13">[10]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yoga_and_contemplative_exercises">Yoga and contemplative exercises</span></h4>
<p>Shankara considered the purity and steadiness of mind achieved in <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a> as an aid to gaining <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a> knowledge, but such yogic state of mind cannot in itself give rise to such knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[95]</a></sup> To Shankara, that knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> springs only from inquiry into the teachings of the Upanishads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[96]</a></sup> The method of yoga, encouraged in Shankara's teachings notes Comans, includes withdrawal of mind from sense objects as in Patanjali's system, but it is not complete thought suppression, instead it is a "meditative exercise of withdrawal from the particular and identification with the universal, leading to contemplation of oneself as the most universal, namely, Consciousness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michaelcomans_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-michaelcomans-113">[97]</a></sup> Describing Shankara's style of yogic practice, Comans writes:
</p>
<blockquote><p>the type of yoga which Sankara presents here is a method of merging, as it were, the particular (visesa) into the general (samanya). For example, diverse sounds are merged in the sense of hearing, which has greater generality insofar as the sense of hearing is the locus of all sounds. The sense of hearing is merged into the mind, whose nature consists of thinking about things, and the mind is in turn merged into the intellect, which Sankara then says is made into 'mere cognition' (vijnanamatra); that is, all particular cognitions resolve into their universal, which is cognition as such, thought without any particular object. And that in turn is merged into its universal, mere Consciousness (prajnafnaghana), upon which everything previously referred to ultimately depends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michaelcomans_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-michaelcomans-113">[97]</a></sup> </p></blockquote>
<p>Shankara rejected those yoga system variations that suggest complete thought suppression leads to liberation, as well the view that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shruti" title="Shruti">Shrutis</a> teach liberation as something apart from the knowledge of the oneness of the Self. Knowledge alone and insights relating to true nature of things, taught Shankara, is what liberates. He placed great emphasis on the study of the Upanisads, emphasizing them as necessary and sufficient means to gain Self-liberating knowledge. Sankara also emphasized the need for and the role of <i>Guru</i> (Acharya, teacher) for such knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michaelcomans_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-michaelcomans-113">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Samanvayat_Tatparya_Linga"><i>Samanvayat Tatparya Linga</i></span></h4>
<p>Shankara cautioned against cherrypicking a phrase or verse out of context from Vedic literature, and remarks in the opening chapter of his Brahmasutra-Bhasya that the <i>Anvaya</i> (theme or purport) of any treatise can only be correctly understood if one attends to the <i>Samanvayat Tatparya Linga</i>, that is six characteristics of the text under consideration: (1) the common in <i>Upakrama</i> (introductory statement) and <i>Upasamhara</i> (conclusions); (2) <i>Abhyasa</i> (message repeated); (3) <i>Apurvata</i> (unique proposition or novelty); (4) <i>Phala</i> (fruit or result derived); (5) <i>Arthavada</i> (explained meaning, praised point) and (6) <i>Yukti</i> (verifiable reasoning).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200646–53_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200646–53-115">[99]</a></sup> While this methodology has roots in the theoretical works of <a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyaya</a> school of Hinduism, Shankara consolidated and applied it with his unique exegetical method called <i>Anvaya-Vyatireka</i>, which states that for proper understanding one must "accept only meanings that are compatible with all characteristics" and "exclude meanings that are incompatible with any".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="The_Mahavyakas_-_the_identity_of_.C4.80tman_and_Brahman"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Mahavyakas_-_the_identity_of_Ātman_and_Brahman">The <i>Mahavyakas</i> - the identity of Ātman and Brahman</span></h3>
<p><i>Moksha</i>, liberation from suffering and rebirth and attaining immortality, is attained by disidentification from the body-mind complex and gaining self-knowledge as being in essence <i>Atman</i>, and attaining knowledge of the identity of <i>Atman</i> and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000183_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000183-92">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan200626_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan200626-90">[77]</a></sup> According to Shankara, the individual Ātman and Brahman seem different at the empirical level of reality, but this difference is only an illusion, and at the highest level of reality they are really identical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda199214_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda199214-118">[102]</a></sup> The real self is <i>Sat</i>, "the Existent," that is, <i>Atman-Brahman</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda199212,_172_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda199212,_172-119">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeutsch197349_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeutsch197349-120">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brahman_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brahman-121">[note 16]</a></sup> Whereas the difference between Atman and non-Atman is deemed self-evident, knowledge of the identity of Atman and Brahman is revealed by the <i>shruti</i>, especially the Upanishadic statement <i>tat tvam asi</i>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mahavyakas"><i>Mahavyakas</i></span></h4>
<p>According to Shankara, a large number of Upanishadic statements reveal the identity of <i>Atman</i> and <i>Brahman</i>. In the Advaita Vedanta tradition, four of those statements, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavakyas" title="Mahavakyas">Mahavakyas</a></i>, which are taken literal, in contrast to other statements, have a special importance in revealing this identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Long-122">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBraue198481_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBraue198481-123">[106]</a></sup> They are:
</p>
<ul><li>तत्त्वमसि, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tattvamasi" title="Tattvamasi">tat tvam asi</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Chandogya_Upanishad" title="Chandogya Upanishad">Chandogya VI.8.7</a>. Traditionally rendered as "That Thou Art" (that you are),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrereton1986_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrereton1986-124">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle2008349_note_8.7-16.3_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle2008349_note_8.7-16.3-125">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack201236_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack201236-126">[109]</a></sup> with <i><a href="/wiki/Tattva" title="Tattva">tat</a></i> in Ch.U.6.8.7 referring to <i><a href="/wiki/Satya" title="Satya">sat</a></i>, "the Existent"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner200055_note_9,_57_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner200055_note_9,_57-127">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeutschDalvi20048_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeutschDalvi20048-128">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle2008151-152;_p.349_note_8.7-16.3_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle2008151-152;_p.349_note_8.7-16.3-129">[112]</a></sup>); correctly translated as "That's how [thus] you are,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrereton1986_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrereton1986-124">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack201236_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack201236-126">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1998152_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1998152-130">[113]</a></sup> with <i>tat</i> in Ch.U.6.12.3, it' original location from where it was copied to other verses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrereton1986_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrereton1986-124">[107]</a></sup> referring to "the very nature of all existence as permeated by [the finest essence]"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatawadekar2013203,_note_14_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatawadekar2013203,_note_14-131">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrereton1986107_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrereton1986107-132">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li>अहं ब्रह्मास्मि, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aham_brahm%C4%81smi" title="Aham brahmāsmi">aham brahmāsmi</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brhadaranyaka_Upanishad" title="Brhadaranyaka Upanishad">Brhadāranyaka I.4.10</a>, "I am Brahman," or "I am  Divine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBraue198480_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBraue198480-133">[116]</a></sup></li>
<li>प्रज्ञानं ब्रह्म, <i>prajñānam brahma</i>, <a href="/wiki/Aitareya_Upanishad" title="Aitareya Upanishad">Aitareya V.3</a>, "<i>Prajñānam</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[note 17]</a></sup> <i>is Brahman</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[note 18]</a></sup></li>
<li>अयमात्मा ब्रह्म, <i>ayamātmā brahma</i>, <a href="/wiki/Mandukya_Upanishad" title="Mandukya Upanishad">Mandukya II</a>, "This Atman is Brahman."</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="That_you_are"><i>That you are</i></span></h4>
<p>The longest chapter of Shankara's <i><a href="/wiki/Upadesasahasri" title="Upadesasahasri">Upadesasahasri</a></i>, chapter 18, "That Art Thou," is devoted to considerations on the insight "I am ever-free, the existent" (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sat_(Sanskrit)" title="Sat (Sanskrit)">sat</a></i>), and the identity expressed in <a href="/wiki/Chandogya_Upanishad#Sixth_Prapāṭhaka" title="Chandogya Upanishad">Chandogya Upanishad 6.8.7</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%81kyas" title="Mahāvākyas">mahavakya</a></i> (great sentence) "<i>tat tvam asi</i>", "that thou art."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda199250,_172_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda199250,_172-139">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner200057_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner200057-140">[120]</a></sup> In this statement, according to Shankara, <i>tat</i> refers to <i><a href="/wiki/Satya" title="Satya">Sat</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner200057_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner200057-140">[120]</a></sup> "the Existent"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner200055_note_9,_57_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner200055_note_9,_57-127">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeutschDalvi20048_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeutschDalvi20048-128">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle2008151-152_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle2008151-152-141">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992172,_Up.18.3,_18.6,_18.7_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992172,_Up.18.3,_18.6,_18.7-142">[122]</a></sup> Existence, Being,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShankaraBasya_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShankaraBasya-143">[123]</a></sup> or Brahman,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992172,_Up.18.6_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992172,_Up.18.6-144">[124]</a></sup> the Real, the "Root of the world,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner200057_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner200057-140">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[note 19]</a></sup> the true essence or root or origin of everything that exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeutschDalvi20048_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeutschDalvi20048-128">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle2008151-152_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle2008151-152-141">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShankaraBasya_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-ShankaraBasya-143">[123]</a></sup> "Tvam" refers to one's real I, <i>pratyagatman</i> or inner Self,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner200060,_62_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner200060,_62-146">[125]</a></sup> the "direct Witness within everything,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner200060_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner200060-147">[126]</a></sup> "free from caste, family, and purifying ceremonies,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992218_(up.II.1.24)_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992218_(up.II.1.24)-148">[127]</a></sup> the essence, <i>Atman</i>, which the individual at the core is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxmuller61_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-maxmuller61-149">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[129]</a></sup> As Shankara states in the <i><a href="/wiki/Upadesasahasri" title="Upadesasahasri">Upadesasahasri</a></i>: 
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Up.I.174: "Through such sentences as "Thou art That" one knows one's own <i>Atman</i>, the Witness of all the internal organs." Up.I.18.190: "Through such sentences as "[Thou art] the Existent" [...] right knowledge concerning the inner <i>Atman</i> will become clearer." Up.I.18.193-194: "In the sentence "Thou art That" [...] [t]he word "That" means inner <i>Atman</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992190–192_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992190–192-151">[130]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The statement "tat tvam asi" sheds the false notion that <i>Atman</i> is different from <i>Brahman</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda199291,_219_(Up.II.1.28)_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda199291,_219_(Up.II.1.28)-152">[131]</a></sup> According toNakamura, the non-duality of <i>atman</i> and <i>Brahman</i> "is a famous characteristic of Sankara's thought, but it was already taught by Sundarapandya"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1999675_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1999675-153">[132]</a></sup> (c.600 CE or earlier).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1999176_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1999176-18">[15]</a></sup> Shankara cites Sundarapandya in his comments to <i>Brahma Sutra</i> verse I.1.4:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When the metaphorical or false <i>atman</i> is non-existent, [the ideas of my] child, [my] body are sublated. Therefore, when it is realized that 'I am the existent <i>Brahman, atman<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span></i>, how can anyduty exist?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1999178_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1999178-154">[133]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>From this, and a large number of other accordances, Nakamura concludes that Shankar was not an original thinker, but "a synthesizer of existing Advaita and the rejuvenator, as well as a defender, of ancient learning."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1999679_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1999679-155">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Meditation_on_the_Mahavyaka">Meditation on the <i>Mahavyaka</i></span></h4>
<p>In the <i>Upadesasahasri Shankara</i>, Shankara is ambivalent on the need for meditation on the Upanishadic <i>mahavyaka</i>. He states that "right knowledge arises at the moment of hearing,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992182_(Up.I.18.103-104)_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992182_(Up.I.18.103-104)-156">[135]</a></sup> and rejects <i>prasamcaksa</i> or <i>prasamkhyana</i> meditation, that is, meditation on the meaning of the sentences, and in Up.II.3 recommends <i>parisamkhyana</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992173-174_(Up.I.18.9-19),_p.196_note_13_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992173-174_(Up.I.18.9-19),_p.196_note_13-157">[136]</a></sup> separating <i>Atman</i> from everything that is not <i>Atman</i>, that is, the sense-objects and sense-organs, and the pleasant and unpleasant things and merit and demerit connected with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992251-253_(Up.II.3)_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992251-253_(Up.II.3)-158">[137]</a></sup> Yet, Shankara then concludes with declaring that only <i>Atman</i> exists, stating that "all the sentences of the <i>Upanishads</i> concerning non-duality of <i>Atman</i> should be fully contemplated, should be contemplated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992253_(Up.II.3)_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992253_(Up.II.3)-159">[138]</a></sup> As Mayeda states, "how they [<i>prasamcaksa</i> or <i>prasamkhyana</i> versus <i>parisamkhyana</i>] differ from each other in not known."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992196_note_13_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992196_note_13-160">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Prasamkhyana</i> was advocated by Mandana Misra,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1991155_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1991155-161">[140]</a></sup> the older contemporary of Shankara who was the most influential Advaitin until the 10th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2002128_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2002128-162">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34-34">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King2002_MandanaMisra_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-King2002_MandanaMisra-163">[note 20]</a></sup> "According to Mandana, the <i>mahavakyas</i> are incapable, by themselves, of bringing about <i>brahmajnana</i>. The <i>Vedanta-vakyas</i> convey an indirect knowledge which is made direct only by deep meditation (<i>prasamkhyana</i>). The latter is a continuous contemplation of the purport of the <i>mahavakyas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1991155-156_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1991155-156-164">[142]</a></sup> Vācaspati Miśra, a student of Mandana Misra, agreed with Mandana Misra, and their stance is defended by the Bhamati-school, founded by Vācaspati Miśra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1991156_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1991156-165">[143]</a></sup> In contrast, the <a href="/wiki/Vivarana" title="Vivarana">Vivarana</a> school founded by Prakasatman (c. 1200–1300)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200240_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200240-166">[144]</a></sup> follows Shankara closely, arguing that the <i>mahavakyas</i> are the direct cause of gaining knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECenkner199595_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECenkner199595-167">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renouncement_of_ritualism">Renouncement of ritualism</span></h4>
<p>Shankara, in his text <i>Upadesasahasri</i>, discourages ritual worship such as oblations to <i>Deva</i> (God), because that assumes the Self within is different from the <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ritualism_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-ritualism-20">[note 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mookerji_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mookerji-21">[note 4]</a></sup> The "doctrine of difference" is wrong, asserts Shankara, because, "he who knows the Brahman is one and he is another, does not know Brahman".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter2008219–221_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter2008219–221-170">[148]</a></sup> The false notion that <i>Atman</i> is different from <i>Brahman</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda199291,_219_(Up.II.1.28)_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda199291,_219_(Up.II.1.28)-152">[131]</a></sup> is connected with the novice's conviction that (<i>Upadesasaharsi</i> II.1.25)
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...I am one [and] He is another; I am ignorant, experience pleasure and pain, am bound and a transmigrator [whereas] he is essentially different from me, the god not subject to transmigration. By worshipping Him with oblation, offerings, homage and the like through the [performance of] the actions prescribed for [my] class and stage of life, I wish to get out of the ocean of transmigratory existence. How am I he?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda199291,_218_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda199291,_218-171">[149]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Recognizing oneself as "the Existent-<i>Brahman</i>," which is mediated by scriptural teachings, is contrasted with the notion of "I act," which is mediated by relying on sense-perception and the like.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992172-173_(Up.I.18.3-8)_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992172-173_(Up.I.18.3-8)-172">[150]</a></sup> According to Shankara, the statement "Thou art That" "remove[s] the delusion of a hearer,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992183_(Up.I.18.99-100)_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992183_(Up.I.18.99-100)-173">[151]</a></sup> "so through sentences as "Thou art That" one knows one's own <i>Atman</i>, the witness of all internal organs,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992190_(Up.I.18.174)_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992190_(Up.I.18.174)-174">[152]</a></sup> and not from any actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992192_(Up.I.18.196-197);_p._195_(Up.I.18.2019)_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda1992192_(Up.I.18.196-197);_p._195_(Up.I.18.2019)-175">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[note 21]</a></sup> With this realization, the performance of rituals is prohibited, "since [the use of] rituals and their requisites is contradictory to the realization of the identity [of <i>Atman</i>] with the highest <i>Atman</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda199285,_220_(Up.II.1.30)_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda199285,_220_(Up.II.1.30)-178">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Shankara also asserts that Self-knowledge is realized when one's mind is purified by an ethical life that observes <a href="/wiki/Yamas" title="Yamas">Yamas</a> such as <i>Ahimsa</i> (non-injury, non-violence to others in body, mind and thoughts) and <a href="/wiki/Niyama" title="Niyama">Niyamas</a>. Rituals and rites such as <a href="/wiki/Yajna" title="Yajna">yajna</a> (a fire ritual), asserts Shankara, can help draw and prepare the mind for the journey to Self-knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200692–93_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200692–93-179">[156]</a></sup> He emphasizes the need for ethics such as <a href="/wiki/Akrodha" title="Akrodha">Akrodha</a> and <i>Yamas</i> during <a href="/wiki/Brahmacharya" title="Brahmacharya">Brahmacharya</a>, stating the lack of ethics as causes that prevent students from attaining knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda200692–93_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda200692–93-179">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter2008218–219_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter2008218–219-180">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences_of_Mahayana_Buddhism">Influences of Mahayana Buddhism</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta#Buddhist_influences" title="Advaita Vedanta">Buddhist influences on Advaita Vedanta</a></div>
<p>Shankara's Vedanta shows similarities with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahayana_Buddhism" title="Mahayana Buddhism">Mahayana Buddhism</a>; opponents have even accused Shankara of being a "crypto-Buddhist,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isaeva14_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-isaeva14-25">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiderman1978405–413_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiderman1978405–413-24">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1995183_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1995183-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crypto-Buddhist_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-crypto-Buddhist-27">[note 5]</a></sup> a qualification which is rejected by the Advaita Vedanta tradition, given the differences between these two schools. According to Shankara, a major difference between Advaita and Mahayana Buddhism are their views on Atman and Brahman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199360,_145–154_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199360,_145–154-28">[22]</a></sup> According to both Loy and Jayatilleke, more differences can be discerned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-david_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-david-181">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Similarities_and_influences">Similarities and influences</span></h3>
<p>Despite Shankara's criticism of certain schools of Mahayana Buddhism, Shankara's philosophy shows strong similarities with the Mahayana Buddhist philosophy which he attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shcherbatsky_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Shcherbatsky-22">[17]</a></sup> According to S.N. Dasgupta,
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Shankara and his followers borrowed much of their dialectic form of criticism from the Buddhists. His <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> was very much like the <a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C5%ABnyat%C4%81" title="Śūnyatā">sunya</a> of Nagarjuna [...] The debts of Shankara to the self-luminosity of the Vijnanavada Buddhism can hardly be overestimated. There seems to be much truth in the accusations against Shankara by <a href="/wiki/Vijnanabhiksu" title="Vijnanabhiksu">Vijnana Bhiksu</a> and others that he was a hidden Buddhist himself.
