I am led to think that Shankara's philosophy is largely a compound of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yogacara" title="Yogacara">Vijnanavada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madhyamaka" title="Madhyamaka">Sunyavada</a> Buddhism with the Upanisad notion of the permanence of self superadded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDasgupta1997494_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDasgupta1997494-23">[18]</a></sup> </p></blockquote>
<p>According to Mudgal, Shankara's Advaita and the Buddhist Madhyamaka view of ultimate reality are compatible because they are both transcendental, indescribable, non-dual and only arrived at through a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Via_negativa" title="Via negativa">via negativa</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Neti_neti" title="Neti neti">neti neti</a>). Mudgal concludes therefore that
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... the difference between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shunyata" title="Shunyata">Sunyavada</a> (Mahayana) philosophy of Buddhism and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advaita" title="Advaita">Advaita</a> philosophy of Hinduism may be a matter of emphasis, not of kind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[160]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Some Hindu scholars criticized Advaita for its <i>Maya</i> and non-theistic doctrinal similarities with Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhaling19791–42_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaling19791–42-185">[162]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ramanuja" title="Ramanuja">Ramanuja</a>, the founder of Vishishtadvaita Vedānta, accused Adi Shankara of being a <i>Prachanna Bauddha</i>, that is, a "crypto-Buddhist",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiderman1978405–413_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiderman1978405–413-24">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isaeva14_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-isaeva14-25">[20]</a></sup> and someone who was undermining theistic <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a> devotionalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhaling19791–42_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaling19791–42-185">[162]</a></sup> The non-Advaita scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_(philosopher)" title="Bhāskara (philosopher)">Bhaskara</a> of the Bhedabheda Vedānta tradition, similarly around 800 CE, accused Shankara's Advaita as "this despicable broken down Mayavada that has been chanted by the Mahayana Buddhists", and a school that is undermining the ritual duties set in Vedic orthodoxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhaling19791–42_185-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaling19791–42-185">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differences">Differences</span></h3>
<p>The qualification of "crypto-Buddhist" is rejected by the Advaita Vedanta tradition, highlighting their respective views on <i>Atman</i>, <i>Anatta</i> and <i>Brahman</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199360,_145–154_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199360,_145–154-28">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-self_no-self_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-self_no-self-29">[note 6]</a></sup> There are differences in the conceptual means of "liberation." <a href="/wiki/Nirvana" title="Nirvana">Nirvana</a>, a term more often used in Buddhism, is the liberating 'blowing out' of craving, aided by the realization and acceptance that there is no Self (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatman" title="Anatman">anatman</a>) as the center of perception, craving, and delusion. <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">Moksha</a>, a term more common in Hinduism, is the similar liberating release from craving and ignorance, yet aided by the realization and acceptance that one's inner Self is not a personal 'ego-self', but an Universal Self.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-david_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-david-181">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_and_cultural_impact">Historical and cultural impact</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hinduism" title="History of Hinduism">History of Hinduism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SankaraSthampaMandapam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3091" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/SankaraSthampaMandapam.jpg/170px-SankaraSthampaMandapam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/SankaraSthampaMandapam.jpg/255px-SankaraSthampaMandapam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/SankaraSthampaMandapam.jpg/340px-SankaraSthampaMandapam.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SankaraSthampaMandapam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adi Sankara Keerthi Sthampa Mandapam, Kalady, Kochi</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_context">Historical context</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">History of India</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hinduism" title="History of Hinduism">History of Hinduism</a></div>
<p>Shankara lived in the time of the great "Late classical Hinduism",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200441–43_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200441–43-187">[164]</a></sup> which lasted from 650 till 1100 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200441–43_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200441–43-187">[164]</a></sup> This era was one of political instability that followed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gupta_dynasty" title="Gupta dynasty">Gupta dynasty</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Harsha" title="King Harsha">King Harsha</a> of the 7th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoller201299–108_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoller201299–108-188">[165]</a></sup> power became decentralised in India. Several larger kingdoms emerged, with "countless vasal states".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200441_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200441-189">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[note 22]</a></sup> The kingdoms were ruled via a feudal system. Smaller kingdoms were dependent on the protection of the larger kingdoms. "The great king was remote, was exalted and deified",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200441_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200441-189">[166]</a></sup> as reflected in the <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">Tantric</a> <a href="/wiki/Mandala" title="Mandala">Mandala</a>, which could also depict the king as the centre of the mandala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite200025–28_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite200025–28-191">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>The disintegration of central power also lead to regionalisation of religiosity, and religious rivalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442-192">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[note 23]</a></sup> Local cults and languages were enhanced, and the influence of "Brahmanic ritualistic Hinduism"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442-192">[168]</a></sup> was diminished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442-192">[168]</a></sup> Rural and devotional movements arose, along with <a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shaivism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vaisnavism" title="Vaisnavism">Vaisnavism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">Tantra</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442_192-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442-192">[168]</a></sup> though "sectarian groupings were only at the beginning of their development".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442_192-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442-192">[168]</a></sup> Religious movements had to compete for recognition by the local lords,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442_192-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442-192">[168]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and various traditions within Hinduism were competing for members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frankwhaling_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-frankwhaling-196">[171]</a></sup> Buddhism in particular had emerged as a powerful influence in India's spiritual traditions in the first 700 years of the 1st millennium CE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoller201299–108_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoller201299–108-188">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter20081–21,_103–119_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter20081–21,_103–119-197">[172]</a></sup> but lost its position after the 8th century, and began to disappear in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442_192-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200442-192">[168]</a></sup> This was reflected in the change of puja-ceremonies at the courts in the 8th century, where Hindu gods replaced the Buddha as the "supreme, imperial deity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[note 24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_Hinduism">Influence on Hinduism</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_view">Traditional view</span></h4>
<p>Shankara has an unparallelled status in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a>. Hagiographies from the 14th-17th century portray him as a victor who travelled all over India to help restore the study of the Vedas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[173]</a></sup> According to Frank Whaling, "Hindus of the Advaita persuasion (and others too) have seen in Sankara the one who restored the Hindu <i>dharma</i> against the attacks of the Buddhists (and Jains) and in the process helped to drive Buddhism out of India."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[174]</a></sup> His teachings and <a href="/wiki/Sampradaya" title="Sampradaya">tradition</a> are central to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smartism" title="Smartism">Smartism</a> and have influenced <a href="/wiki/Sant_Mat" title="Sant Mat">Sant Mat</a> lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[175]</a></sup> Tradition portrays him as the one who reconciled the various sects (Vaishnavism, Shaivism, and Saktism) with the introduction of the <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Panchayatana_puja" title="Panchayatana puja">Pañcāyatana</a></i></span> form of <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">worship</a>, the simultaneous worship of five deities – Ganesha, Surya, Vishnu, Shiva and Devi, arguing that all deities were but different forms of the one <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>, the invisible Supreme Being,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[176]</a></sup> implying that Advaita Vedanta stoos above all other traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKruijfSahoo2014105_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKruijfSahoo2014105-203">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Koller, Shankara, and his contemporaries, made a significant contribution in understanding Buddhism and the ancient Vedic traditions, then transforming the extant ideas, particularly reforming the Vedanta tradition of Hinduism, making it India's most important "spiritual tradition" for more than a thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoller201299_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoller201299-204">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[note 25]</a></sup> Benedict Ashley credits Adi Shankara for unifying two seemingly disparate philosophical doctrines in Hinduism, namely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jivatma" title="Jivatma">Atman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_assessment">Critical assessment</span></h4>
<p>Scholars have questioned Shankara's early influence in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30-36">[29]</a></sup> The Buddhist scholar <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._King" title="Richard E. King">Richard E. King</a> states,
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Although it is common to find Western scholars and Hindus arguing that Sankaracarya was the most influential and important figure in the history of Hindu intellectual thought, this does not seem to be justified by the historical evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2001128_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2001128-32">[25]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h5><span id="Prominence_of_Ma.E1.B9.87.E1.B8.8Dana_Mi.C5.9Bra_.28until_10th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prominence_of_Maṇḍana_Miśra_(until_10th_century)">Prominence of Maṇḍana Miśra (until 10th century)</span></h5>
<p>According to Clark, "Sankara was relatively unknown during his life-time, and probably for several centuries after, as there is no mention of him in Buddhist or jain sources for centuries; nor is he mentioned by other important philosophers of the ninth and tenth centuries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark2006217_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark2006217-35">[28]</a></sup> According to King and Roodurmun, until the 10th century Shankara was overshadowed by his older contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ma%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8Dana_Mi%C5%9Bra" title="Maṇḍana Miśra">Mandana-Misra</a>, the latter considered to be the major representative of Advaita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2001128_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2001128-32">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34-34">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ma%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8Dana_Mi%C5%9Bra" title="Maṇḍana Miśra">Maṇḍana Miśra</a>, an older contemporary of Shankara,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200229_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200229-31">[24]</a></sup> was a Mimamsa scholar and a follower of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kumarila" title="Kumarila">Kumarila</a>, but also wrote a seminal text on Advaita that has survived into the modern era, the <i>Brahma-siddhi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200231_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200231-207">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[181]</a></sup> The "theory of error" set forth in the <i>Brahma-siddhi</i> became the normative Advaita Vedanta theory of error,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200232_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200232-209">[182]</a></sup> and for a couple of centuries he was the most influential Vedantin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2002128_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2002128-162">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200233–34-34">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King2002_MandanaMisra_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-King2002_MandanaMisra-163">[note 20]</a></sup> His student <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vachaspati_Mi%C5%9Bra" title="Vachaspati Miśra">Vachaspati Miśra</a>, who is believed to have been an incarnation of Shankara to popularize the Advaita view,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200234_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200234-210">[183]</a></sup> wrote the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhamati" title="Bhamati">Bhamati</a></i>, a commentary on Shankara's <i>Brahma Sutra Bhashya</i>, and the <i>Brahmatattva-samiksa</i>, a commentary on Mandana Mishra's <i>Brahma-siddhi</i>. His thought was mainly inspired by Mandana Miśra, and harmonises Shankara's thought with that of Mandana Miśra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200235_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200235-211">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BhamatiVivarana_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-BhamatiVivarana-212">[web 3]</a></sup> The Bhamati school takes an ontological approach. It sees the <a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">Jiva</a> as the source of avidya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BhamatiVivarana_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-BhamatiVivarana-212">[web 3]</a></sup> It sees yogic practice and contemplation as the main factor in the acquirement of liberation, while the study of the Vedas and reflection are additional factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing199956_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing199956-213">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200237_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200237-214">[186]</a></sup> The later Advaita Vedanta tradition incorporated Maṇḍana Miśra into the Shankara-fold, by identifying him with <a href="/wiki/Sure%C5%9Bvara" title="Sureśvara">Sureśvara</a> (9th century),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-karlpottermms_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-karlpottermms-215">[187]</a></sup> believing that Maṇḍana Miśra became a disciple of Shankara after a public debate which Shankara won.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200231_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoodurmun200231-207">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma1997290–291_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1997290–291-216">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satchidanandendra_Sarasvati" title="Satchidanandendra Sarasvati">Satchidanandendra Sarasvati</a>, "almost all the later Advaitins were influenced by Mandana Misra and <a href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_(Bhedabheda_Vedanta)" title="Bhāskara (Bhedabheda Vedanta)">Bhaskara</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESatchidanandendra_Sarasvati19976_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESatchidanandendra_Sarasvati19976-217">[189]</a></sup> He argues that most of post-Shankara Advaita Vedanta actually deviates from Shankara, and that only his student Suresvara, who's had little influence, represents Shankara correctly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter20066–7_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter20066–7-218">[190]</a></sup> In this view, Shankara's influential student Padmapada misunderstood Shankara, while his views were manitained by the Suresvara school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter20066–7_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter20066–7-218">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[note 26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span id="Vaishnavite_Vedanta_.2810th-14th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaishnavite_Vedanta_(10th-14th_century)">Vaishnavite Vedanta (10th-14th century)</span></h5>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hajime_Nakamura" title="Hajime Nakamura">Hajime Nakamura</a> states that prior to Shankara, views similar to his already existed, but did not occupy a dominant position within the Vedanta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004690_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004690-220">[191]</a></sup> Until the 11th century, Vedanta itself was a peripheral school of thought;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson2010157;_229_note_57_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson2010157;_229_note_57-221">[192]</a></sup> Vedanta became a major influence when it was utilized by various sects of Hinduism to ground their doctrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004691-693_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004691-693-222">[193]</a></sup> The early Vedanta scholars were from the upper classes of society, well-educated in traditional culture. They formed a social elite, "sharply distinguished from the general practitioners and theologians of Hinduism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004693_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004693-223">[194]</a></sup> Their teachings were "transmitted among a small number of selected intellectuals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004693_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004693-223">[194]</a></sup> Works of the early Vedanta schools do not contain references to Vishnu or Shiva.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004692_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004692-224">[195]</a></sup> It was only after Shankara that "the theologians of the various sects of Hinduism utilized Vedanta philosophy to a greater or lesser degree to form the basis of their doctrines,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004691_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004691-225">[196]</a></sup> whereby "its theoretical influence upon the whole of Indian society became final and definitive."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura2004693_223-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura2004693-223">[194]</a></sup> Examples are <a href="/wiki/Ramanuja" title="Ramanuja">Ramanuja</a> (11th c.), who aligned <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">bhakti</a>, "the major force in the religions of Hinduism," with philosophical thought, meanwhile rejecting Shankara's views,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_Ramanuja_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB_Ramanuja-226">[web 4]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Nath" title="Nath">Nath</a>-tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuerstein1978_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuerstein1978-227">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span id="Vijayanagara_Empire_and_Vidyaranya_.2814th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vijayanagara_Empire_and_Vidyaranya_(14th_century)">Vijayanagara Empire and Vidyaranya (14th century)</span></h5>
<p>In medieval times, Advaita Vedanta position as most influential Hindu <i>darsana</i> started to take shape, as Advaitins in the Vijayanagara Empire competed for patronage from the royal court, and tried to convert others to their sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoker201655-56_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoker201655-56-228">[198]</a></sup> It is only during this period that the historical fame and cultural influence of Shankara and Advaita Vedanta was established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30-36">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake_Michael199260–62_with_notes_6,_7_and_8_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake_Michael199260–62_with_notes_6,_7_and_8-229">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson2010178–183_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson2010178–183-230">[200]</a></sup> Many of Shankara's biographies were created and published in and after the 14th century, such as Vidyaranya's widely cited <i>Śankara-vijaya</i>. <a href="/wiki/Vidyaranya" title="Vidyaranya">Vidyaranya</a>, also known as Madhava, who was the 12th Jagadguru of the Śringeri Śarada Pītham from 1380 to 1386<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[201]</a></sup> and a minister in the Vijayanagara Empire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-talbot-232">[202]</a></sup> inspired the re-creation of the Hindu <a href="/wiki/Vijayanagara_Empire" title="Vijayanagara Empire">Vijayanagara Empire</a> of South India. This may have been in response to the devastation caused by the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30-36">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake_Michael199260–62_with_notes_6,_7_and_8_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake_Michael199260–62_with_notes_6,_7_and_8-229">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson2010178–183_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson2010178–183-230">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-talbot-232">[202]</a></sup> but his efforts were also targeted at Sri Vaishnava groups, especially <i>Visishtadvaita</i>, which was dominant in territories conquered by the Vijayanagara Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoker201655_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoker201655-233">[203]</a></sup> Furthermore, sects competed for patronage from the royal court, and tried to convert others to their own sectarian system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoker201655-56_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoker201655-56-228">[198]</a></sup> Vidyaranya and his brothers, note Paul Hacker and other scholars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30-36">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake_Michael199260–62_with_notes_6,_7_and_8_229-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake_Michael199260–62_with_notes_6,_7_and_8-229">[199]</a></sup> wrote extensive Advaitic commentaries on the Vedas and Dharma to make "the authoritative literature of the Aryan religion" more accessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529-234">[204]</a></sup> Vidyaranya was an influential Advaitin, and he created legends to turn Shankara, whose elevated philosophy had no appeal to gain widespread popularity, into a "divine folk-hero who spread his teaching through his <i>digvijaya</i> ("universal conquest") all over India like a victorious conqueror."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529-234">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund1998177_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund1998177-235">[205]</a></sup> In his <a href="/wiki/Doxography" title="Doxography">doxography</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Sarva-Darsana-Sangraha" title="Sarva-Darsana-Sangraha">Sarvadarśanasaṅgraha</a></i> ("Summary of all views") Vidyaranya presented Shankara's teachings as the summit of all <i>darsanas</i>, presenting the other <i>darsanas</i> as partial truths which converged in Shankara's teachings, which was regarded to be the most inclusive system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson2010160–162_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson2010160–162-236">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529_234-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529-234">[204]</a></sup> The Vaishanava traditions of Dvaita and Visishtadvaita were not classified as Vedanta, and placed just above Buddhism and Jainism, reflecting the threat they posed for Vidyaranya's Advaita allegiance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson2010160_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson2010160-237">[207]</a></sup> Bhedabheda wasn't mentioned at all, "literally written out of the history of Indian philosophy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson2010161_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson2010161-238">[208]</a></sup> Such was the influence of the <i>Sarvadarśanasaṅgraha</i>, that early Indologists also regarded Advaita Vedanta as the most accurate interpretation of the Upanishads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson2010160_237-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson2010160-237">[207]</a></sup> And Vidyaranya founded a <i>matha</i>, proclaiming that it was established by Shankara himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529_234-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529-234">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund1998177_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund1998177-235">[205]</a></sup> Vidyaranya enjoyed royal support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-talbot-232">[202]</a></sup> and his sponsorship and methodical efforts helped establish Shankara as a rallying symbol of values, spread historical and cultural influence of Shankara's Vedānta philosophies, and establish monasteries (<i>mathas</i>) to expand the cultural influence of Shankara and Advaita Vedānta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30-36">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span id="Neo-Vedanta_.2819-20th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Vedanta_(19-20th_century)">Neo-Vedanta (19-20th century)</span></h5>
<p>Shankara's position was further established in the 19th and 20th-century, when <a href="/wiki/Neo-Vedanta" title="Neo-Vedanta">neo-Vedantins</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">Orientalists</a> elevated Advaita Vedanta "as the connecting theological thread that united Hinduism into a single religious tradition."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2001129_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2001129-239">[209]</a></sup> Shankara became "an iconic representation of Hindu religion and culture," despite the fact that most Hindus do not adhere to Advaita Vedanta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2001129-130_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2001129-130-240">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Digvijaya_-_.22The_conquests_of_Shankara.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Digvijaya_-_"The_conquests_of_Shankara"'><i>Digvijaya</i> - "The conquests of Shankara"</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Digvijaya_(conquest)" title="Digvijaya (conquest)">Digvijaya</a></div>
<p>There are at least fourteen different known <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographies</a> of Adi Shankara's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5-60">[51]</a></sup> These, as well as other hagiographical works on Shankara, were written many centuries to a thousand years after Shankara's death,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande201135_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande201135-241">[211]</a></sup> in Sanskrit and non-Sanskrit languages, and the hagiographies are filled with legends and fiction, often mutually contradictory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5-60">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[note 27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of these are called the <i>Śankara Vijaya</i> ('The conquests (<i><a href="/wiki/Digvijaya_(conquest)" title="Digvijaya (conquest)">digvijaya</a></i>) of Shankara'), while some are called <i>Guruvijaya</i>, <i>Sankarabhyudaya</i> and <i>Shankaracaryacarita</i>. Of these, the <i>Brhat-Sankara-Vijaya</i> by Citsukha is the oldest hagiography but only available in excerpts, while <i>Sankaradigvijaya</i> by Mādhava (17th c.) and <i>Sankaravijaya</i> by Anandagiri are the most cited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5_60-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5-60">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82-44">[37]</a></sup> Other significant hagiographies are the <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Cidvilāsīya Śaṅkara Vijayaṃ</i></span></i> (of Cidvilāsa, c. between the 15th and 17th centuries), and the <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Keraļīya Śaṅkara Vijayaṃ</i></span></i> (of the Kerala region, extant from c. the 17th century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[213]</a></sup>}
</p><p>Scholars note that one of the most cited Shankara hagiographies, Anandagiri's, includes stories and legends about historically different people, but all bearing the same name of Sri Shankaracarya or also referred to as Shankara but likely meaning more ancient scholars with names such as Vidya-sankara, Sankara-misra and Sankara-nanda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82-44">[37]</a></sup> Some hagiographies are probably written by those who sought to create a historical basis for their rituals or theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82-44">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande201135_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande201135-241">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adi_shankara.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="547" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Adi_shankara.jpg/170px-Adi_shankara.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Adi_shankara.jpg/255px-Adi_shankara.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Adi_shankara.jpg/340px-Adi_shankara.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adi_shankara.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Murti of Shankara at his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam%C4%81dhi" title="Samādhi">Samadhi</a> Mandir, behind <a href="/wiki/Kedarnath_Temple" title="Kedarnath Temple">Kedarnath Temple</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Kedarnath" title="Kedarnath">Kedarnath</a>, India</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adi_Sankara_at_SAT_Temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Adi_Sankara_at_SAT_Temple.jpg/220px-Adi_Sankara_at_SAT_Temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Adi_Sankara_at_SAT_Temple.jpg/330px-Adi_Sankara_at_SAT_Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Adi_Sankara_at_SAT_Temple.jpg/440px-Adi_Sankara_at_SAT_Temple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adi_Sankara_at_SAT_Temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Murti of Shankara at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SAT_Temple" title="SAT Temple">SAT Temple</a> in Santa Cruz, California</div></div></div>
<p>According to the oldest hagiographies, Shankara was born in the southern Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>, in a village named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaladi" title="Kaladi">Kaladi</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5_60-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5-60">[51]</a></sup> sometimes spelled as Kalati or Karati.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[note 28]</a></sup> He was born to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nambudiri_Brahmin" title="Nambudiri Brahmin">Nambudiri Brahmin</a> parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[217]</a></sup> His parents were an aged, childless, couple who led a devout life of service to the poor. They named their child Shankara, meaning "giver of prosperity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[218]</a></sup> His father died while Shankara was very young.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5_60-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5-60">[51]</a></sup> Shankara's <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upanayanam" title="Upanayanam">upanayanam</a></i></span>, the initiation into student-life, had to be delayed due to the death of his father, and was then performed by his mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankara's hagiography describe him as someone who was attracted to the life of <a href="/wiki/Sannyasa" title="Sannyasa">Sannyasa</a> (hermit) from early childhood. His mother disapproved. A story, found in all hagiographies, describe Shankara at age eight going to a river with his mother, <i>Sivataraka</i>, to bathe, and where he is caught by a crocodile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199374–75_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199374–75-252">[220]</a></sup> Shankara called out to his mother to give him permission to become a <i>Sannyasin</i> or else the crocodile will kill him. The mother agrees, Shankara is freed and leaves his home for education. He reaches a Saivite sanctuary along a river in a north-central state of India, and becomes the disciple of a teacher named <a href="/wiki/Govinda_Bhagavatpada" title="Govinda Bhagavatpada">Govinda Bhagavatpada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199374–75_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199374–75-252">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68-253">[221]</a></sup> The stories in various hagiographies diverge in details about the first meeting between Shankara and his <i>Guru</i>, where they met, as well as what happened later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199374–75_252-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199374–75-252">[220]</a></sup> Several texts suggest Shankara schooling with Govindapada happened along the river <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narmada" title="Narmada">Narmada</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omkareshwar" title="Omkareshwar">Omkareshwar</a>, a few place it along river Ganges in Kashi (<a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Varanasi</a>) as well as Badari (<a href="/wiki/Badrinath" title="Badrinath">Badrinath</a> in the Himalayas).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68-253">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hagiographies vary in their description of where he went, who he met and debated and many other details of his life. Most mention Shankara studying the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmasutra" title="Brahmasutra">Brahmasutra</a> with Govindapada, and Shankara authoring several key works in his youth, while he was studying with his teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199376–77_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199376–77-254">[222]</a></sup> It is with his teacher Govinda, that Shankara studied Gaudapadiya Karika, as Govinda was himself taught by Gaudapada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5_60-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20063–5-60">[51]</a></sup> Most also mention a meeting with scholars of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mimamsa" title="Mimamsa">Mimamsa</a> school of Hinduism namely Kumarila and Prabhakara, as well as Mandana and various Buddhists, in <i><a href="/wiki/Shastrartha" title="Shastrartha">Shastrartha</a></i> (an Indian tradition of public philosophical debates attended by large number of people, sometimes with royalty).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68_253-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68-253">[221]</a></sup> Thereafter, the hagiographies about Shankara vary significantly. Different and widely inconsistent accounts of his life include diverse journeys, pilgrimages, public debates, installation of yantras and lingas, as well as the founding of monastic centers in north, east, west and south India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199369–82-44">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68_253-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68-253">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digvijaya_and_disciples"><i>Digvijaya</i> and disciples</span></h3>
<p>While the details and chronology vary, most hagiographies present Shankara as traveling widely within India, Gujarat to Bengal, and participating in public philosophical debates with different orthodox schools of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>, as well as heterodox traditions such as Buddhists, Jains, Arhatas, Saugatas, and <a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Charvakas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande20115–36_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande20115–36-255">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[224]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[225]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The hagiographies credit him with starting several <i>Matha</i> (monasteries), but this is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande20115–36_255-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande20115–36-255">[223]</a></sup> Ten monastic orders in different parts of India are generally attributed to Shankara's travel-inspired <i>Sannyasin</i> schools, each with Advaita notions, of which four have continued in his tradition: Bharati (Sringeri), Sarasvati (Kanchi), Tirtha and Asramin (Dvaraka).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199382–91_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199382–91-258">[226]</a></sup> Other monasteries that record Shankara's visit include Giri, Puri, Vana, Aranya, Parvata and Sagara – all names traceable to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashrama_(stage)" title="Ashrama (stage)">Ashrama</a> system in Hinduism and Vedic literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199382–91_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199382–91-258">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankara had a number of disciple scholars during his travels, including <a href="/wiki/Padmapadacharya" title="Padmapadacharya">Padmapadacharya</a> (also called Sanandana, associated with the text <i>Atma-bodha</i>), <a href="/wiki/Sure%C5%9Bvara" title="Sureśvara">Sureśvaracharya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Totakacharya" title="Totakacharya">Totakacharya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hastamalakacharya" title="Hastamalakacharya">Hastamalakacharya</a>, Chitsukha, Prthividhara, Chidvilasayati, Bodhendra, Brahmendra, Sadananda and others, who authored their own literature on Shankara and Advaita Vedanta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande20115–36_255-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande20115–36-255">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199371–82,_93–94_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199371–82,_93–94-259">[227]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<p>Adi Sankara is believed to have died aged 32, at <a href="/wiki/Kedarnath" title="Kedarnath">Kedarnath</a> in the northern Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>, a Hindu pilgrimage site in the Himalayas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199382–91_258-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaeva199382–91-258">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayeda20066–7-9">[6]</a></sup> Texts say that he was last seen by his disciples behind the Kedarnath temple, walking in the Himalayas until he was not traced. Some texts locate his death in alternate locations such as <a href="/wiki/Kanchipuram" title="Kanchipuram">Kanchipuram</a> (Tamil Nadu) and somewhere in the state of Kerala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68_253-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande201131–32,_also_6–7,_67–68-253">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>A statue of Adi Shankara has been built behind <a href="/wiki/Kedarnath_Temple" title="Kedarnath Temple">Kedarnath Temple</a> to commemorate his life and work as part of the temples redevelopment after the 2013 deluge in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[228]</a></sup> The 12-foot statue inaugurated by Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Narendra_Modi" title="Narendra Modi">Narendra Modi</a> on 5 November 2019, is made of chlorite schist and weighs 35 tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[229]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathas_and_Smarta_tradition">Mathas and Smarta tradition</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dashanami_Sampradaya" title="Dashanami Sampradaya">Dashanami Sampradaya</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vidyashankara_Temple_at_Shringeri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1178" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Vidyashankara_Temple_at_Shringeri.jpg/220px-Vidyashankara_Temple_at_Shringeri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Vidyashankara_Temple_at_Shringeri.jpg/330px-Vidyashankara_Temple_at_Shringeri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Vidyashankara_Temple_at_Shringeri.jpg/440px-Vidyashankara_Temple_at_Shringeri.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vidyashankara_Temple_at_Shringeri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vidyashankara temple at <a href="/wiki/Sringeri_Sharada_Peetham" title="Sringeri Sharada Peetham">Sringeri Sharada Peetham</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shringeri" title="Shringeri">Shringeri</a></div></div></div>
<p>Shankara is regarded as the founder of the <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dashanami_Sampradaya" title="Dashanami Sampradaya">Daśanāmi Sampradāya</a></i></span> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Panchayatana_puja" title="Panchayatana puja">Panchayatana puja</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Shanmata" title="Shanmata"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Ṣaṇmata</i></span></a> of the <a href="/wiki/Smarta_tradition" title="Smarta tradition">Smarta tradition</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dashanami_Sampradaya_and_mathas">Dashanami Sampradaya and mathas</span></h3>
<p>Advaita Vedanta is, at least in the west, primarily known as a philosophical system. But it is also a tradition of <a href="/wiki/Sannyasa" title="Sannyasa">renunciation</a>. Philosophy and renunciation are closely related:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MonasticTradition_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-MonasticTradition-262">[web 5]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Most of the notable authors in the advaita tradition were members of the sannyasa tradition, and both sides of the tradition share the same values, attitudes and metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MonasticTradition_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-MonasticTradition-262">[web 5]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Shankara was a Vaishnavite who came to be presented as an incarnation of <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a> in the 14th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark2006218,_220,_224_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark2006218,_220,_224-263">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MonasticTradition_262-2"><a href="#cite_note-MonasticTradition-262">[web 5]</a></sup> to facilitate the adoption of his teachings by previously Saiva-oriented <i>mathas</i> in the Vijayanagara Empire. From the 14th century onwards hagiographies were composed, in which he is portrayed as establishing the <a href="/wiki/Da%C5%9Ban%C4%81mi_Sampradaya" title="Daśanāmi Sampradaya">Daśanāmi Sampradaya</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark2006224-225_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark2006224-225-264">[231]</a></sup> organizing a section of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dashanami_Sampradaya" title="Dashanami Sampradaya">Ekadandi monks</a> under an umbrella grouping of ten names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MonasticTradition_262-3"><a href="#cite_note-MonasticTradition-262">[web 5]</a></sup> Several other Hindu monastic and Ekadandi traditions remained outside the organisation of the Dasanāmis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[233]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to tradition, Adi Sankara organised the Hindu monks of these ten sects or names under four <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Matha" title="Matha">Maṭhas</a></i></span> (Sanskrit: <span title="Sanskrit-language text"><span lang="sa">मठ</span></span>) (monasteries), with the headquarters at <a href="/wiki/Dv%C4%81rak%C4%81" title="Dvārakā">Dvārakā</a> in the West, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jagannatha_Puri" title="Jagannatha Puri">Jagannatha Puri</a> in the East, <a href="/wiki/Sringeri" title="Sringeri">Sringeri</a> in the South and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Badrikashrama" title="Badrikashrama">Badrikashrama</a> in the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MonasticTradition_262-4"><a href="#cite_note-MonasticTradition-262">[web 5]</a></sup> Each <i>matha</i> was headed by one of his four main disciples, who each continues the Vedanta Sampradaya.
</p><p>According to Paul Hacker, the system may have been initiated by <a href="/wiki/Vidyaranya" title="Vidyaranya">Vidyaranya</a> (14th c.), who may have founded a <i>matha</i>, proclaiming that it was established by Shankara himself, as part of his campaign to propagate Shankara's Advaita Vedanta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529_234-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529-234">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund1998177_235-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund1998177-235">[205]</a></sup> Vidyaranya enjoyed royal support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot_232-3"><a href="#cite_note-talbot-232">[202]</a></sup> and his sponsorship and methodical efforts helped establish Shankara as a rallying symbol of values, spread historical and cultural influence of Shankara's Vedānta philosophies, and establish monasteries (<i>mathas</i>) to expand the cultural influence of Shankara and Advaita Vedānta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30_36-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker199529–30-36">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smarta_Tradition">Smarta Tradition</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smarta_Tradition" title="Smarta Tradition">Smarta Tradition</a></div>
<p>Traditionally, Shankara is regarded as the greatest teacher<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoniger19991017_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoniger19991017-267">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_Prakashan200052_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_Prakashan200052-268">[235]</a></sup> and reformer of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smarta_Tradition" title="Smarta Tradition">Smartism</a> <a href="/wiki/Sampradaya" title="Sampradaya">sampradaya</a>, which is one of four major <i>sampradaya</i> of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERosen2006166_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERosen2006166-269">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_Prakashan200052_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_Prakashan200052-268">[235]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Alf_Hiltebeitel" title="Alf Hiltebeitel">Alf Hiltebeitel</a>, Shankara established the nondualist interpretation of the Upanishads as the touchstone of a revived <i>smarta</i> tradition:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Practically, Shankara fostered a rapprochement between Advaita and <i>smarta</i> orthodoxy, which by his time had not only continued to defend the <i>varnasramadharma</i> theory as defining the path of <i>karman</i>, but had developed the practice of <i>pancayatanapuja</i> ("five-shrine worship") as a solution to varied and conflicting devotional practices. Thus one could worship any one of five deities (Vishnu, Siva, Durga, Surya, Ganesa) as one's <i>istadevata</i> ("deity of choice").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel200229_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel200229-270">[237]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><i>Panchayatana puja</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAST" title="IAST">IAST</a> <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Pañcāyatana pūjā</i></span></i>) is a system of <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)"><i>puja</i></a> (worship) in the Smarta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bühnemann2003p60_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bühnemann2003p60-271">[238]</a></sup> It consists of the worship of five deities set in a <a href="/wiki/Quincunx" title="Quincunx">quincunx</a> pattern,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harle1994p141_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harle1994p141-272">[239]</a></sup> the five deities being <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devi" title="Devi">Devi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a>, and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ishta_Devata" title="Ishta Devata">Ishta Devata</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Kartikeya" title="Kartikeya">Kartikeya</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">Ganesha</a> or any personal god of devotee's preference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood1996p17_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flood1996p17-273">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[241]</a></sup> Sometimes the Ishta Devata is the sixth deity in the mandala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bühnemann2003p60_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bühnemann2003p60-271">[238]</a></sup> while in the <a href="/wiki/Shanmata" title="Shanmata">Shanmata</a> system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[242]</a></sup> <i>Skanda</i>, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Kartikeya" title="Kartikeya">Kartikeya</a></i> and <i>Murugan</i>, is added. Panchayatana puja is a practice that became popular in medieval India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bühnemann2003p60_271-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bühnemann2003p60-271">[238]</a></sup> and has been attributed to Adi Shankara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lexicon_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-lexicon-276">[243]</a></sup> However, archaeological evidence suggests that this practice long predates the birth of Adi Shankara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[note 29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Films">Films</span></h2>
<ul><li><i>Shankaracharya</i> (1927), Indian silent film about Shankara by Kali Prasad Ghosh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-279">[245]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Jagadguru Shrimad Shankaracharya</i> (1928), Indian silent film by Parshwanath Yeshwant Altekar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-279">[245]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Jagadguru Shankaracharya</i> (1955), Indian Hindi film by Sheikh Fattelal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_279-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-279">[245]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1977 <a href="/wiki/Jagadguru_Aadisankaran" title="Jagadguru Aadisankaran">Jagadguru Aadisankaran</a>, a Malayalam film directed by <a href="/wiki/P._Bhaskaran" title="P. Bhaskaran">P. Bhaskaran</a> was released in which <i>Murali Mohan</i> plays the role of Adult Aadi Sankaran and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Master_Raghu" title="Master Raghu">Master Raghu</a> plays childhood.</li>
<li>In 1983 a film directed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.V._Iyer" title="G.V. Iyer">G.V. Iyer</a> named <i><a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankaracharya_(film)" title="Adi Shankaracharya (film)">Adi Shankaracharya</a></i> was premiered, the first film ever made entirely in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> language in which all of Adi Shankaracharya's works were compiled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[246]</a></sup> The movie received the Indian <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Awards" title="National Film Awards">National Film Awards</a> for <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Award_for_Best_Feature_Film" title="National Film Award for Best Feature Film">Best Film</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Award_for_Best_Screenplay" title="National Film Award for Best Screenplay">Best Screenplay</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Award_for_Best_Cinematography" title="National Film Award for Best Cinematography">Best Cinematography</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Award_for_Best_Audiography" title="National Film Award for Best Audiography">Best Audiography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31staward_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-31staward-281">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31stawardPDF_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-31stawardPDF-282">[248]</a></sup></li>
<li>On 15 August 2013, <a href="/wiki/Jagadguru_Adi_Shankara" title="Jagadguru Adi Shankara">Jagadguru Adi Shankara</a> was released in an Indian Telugu-language biographical film written and directed by J. K. Bharavi and was later dubbed in Kannada with the same title, by Upendra giving narration for the Kannada dubbed version</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-columns-2">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Modern scholarship places Shankara in the earlier part of the 8th century CE (c. 700–750).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000163_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000163-2">[2]</a></sup> Earlier generations of scholars proposed 788–820 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans2000163_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans2000163-2">[2]</a></sup> Other proposals are 686–718 CE,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> 44 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keshava_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keshava-3">[3]</a></sup> or as early as 509–477 BCE.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He is also known as Shankara Bhagavatpada (<span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Śaṅkara Bhagavatpāda</i></span>), Shankara Bhagavatpadacharya (<span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Śaṅkara Bhagavatpādācārya</i></span>) or simply <a href="/wiki/Shankaracharya" title="Shankaracharya">Shankaracharya</a>, sometimes spelled Sankaracharya.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ritualism-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ritualism_20-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ritualism_20-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ritualism_20-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Shankara, himself, had renounced all religious ritual acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter200816_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter200816-168">[146]</a></sup><br/>For an example of Shankara's reasoning "why rites and ritual actions should be given up", see Karl Potter on p. 220;<br/>Elsewhere, Shankara's <i>Bhasya</i> on various Upanishads repeat "give up rituals and rites", see for example <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/Brihadaranyaka.Upanishad.Shankara.Bhashya.by.Swami.Madhavananda#page/n375/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">Shankara's Bhasya on Brihadaranyaka Upanishad</a> pp. 348–350, 754–757&lt;/ref&gt;</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Mookerji-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Mookerji_21-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mookerji_21-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mookerji_21-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare <a href="#CITEREFMookerji2011">Mookerji 2011</a> on <a href="/wiki/Sv%C4%81dhy%C4%81ya" title="Svādhyāya">Svādhyāya</a> (Vedic learning). <a href="#CITEREFMookerji2011">Mookerji (2011</a>, pp. 29–31) notes that the Rigveda, and Sayana's commentary, contain passages criticizing as fruitless mere recitation of the <i>Ŗik</i> (words) without understanding their inner meaning or essence, the knowledge of <i>dharma</i> and <i>Parabrahman</i>. <a href="#CITEREFMookerji2011">Mookerji (2011</a>, pp. 29, 34) concludes that in the Rigvedic education of the mantras "the contemplation and comprehension of <a href="/wiki/Nirukta" title="Nirukta">their meaning</a> was considered as more important and vital to education than their mere mechanical repetition and correct pronunciation." <a href="#CITEREFMookerji2011">Mookerji (2011</a>, p. 35) refers to Sayana as stating that "the mastery of texts, <i>akshara-praptī</i>, is followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a>-<a href="/wiki/Buddhi" title="Buddhi">bodha</a></i>, perception of their meaning." (<i><a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">Artha</a></i> may also mean "goal, purpose or essence," depending on the context. See:
<a class="external text" href="http://spokensanskrit.de/index.php?tinput=artha&amp;direction=SE&amp;script=HK&amp;link=yes&amp;beginning=0" rel="nofollow">Sanskrit English Dictionary</a> University of Kloen, Germany (2009); Karl Potter (1998), Encyclopedia of Indian Philosophies, Volume 4, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Lock-green.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .id-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px .cs1-kern-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right{padding-right:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .citation .mw-selflink{font-weight:inherit}</style><a class="mw-redirect"
